Jamison Brereton

AsÃÅvins
Agastya MaitrƒÅvaru·πái
10 verses: tri·π£·π≠ubh
Agastya‚Äôs trickery and virtuosic obscurity are fully on display in this hymn. As a consequence we are uncertain about the correctness of many details of our interpretation, but puzzling out the hymn provides an exhilarating ride‚Äîper haps appropriately, since the hymn begins and ends with the A≈õvins‚Äô chariot-drive around the cosmic spaces. This ring composition is signaled by the responsions between verses 1 and 10: the chariot ‚Äúflies around‚Äù the airy realms and the (cosmic) floods in 1ab and ‚Äúspeeds around‚Äù heaven in 10c (a parallel with a clever twist in the Sanskrit, where the verb ‚Äúflies‚Äù [dƒ´ÃÅyat] is a close phonological match with the word for ‚Äúheaven‚Äù [dyƒÅÃÅm]), and a physical feature of the chariot, its wheel-rims or fellies, is mentioned in both verses, but with different words (pav√≠ 1c, -nemi 10c). This ring composition provides a structure in which both the A≈õvins‚Äô journey to and participation in the sacrifice and the poet‚Äôs exploration of the boundaries of language can be anchored.
In our view the flying journey begun in verse 1 brings the A≈õvins to the sacrifi cial ground and its ritual fire in verse 2, a fire that is itself characterized as having ‚Äúwide flight,‚Äù that is, as physically spreading out and up when kindled. The next two verses depict several of the A≈õvins‚Äô earlier deeds as creating a reciprocal benefit for them at the current sacrifice. Verse 3 notes that they (primordially) put the milk in cows, a fact of nature that is often marveled at in the RÃ•gveda‚Äîthat a ‚Äúcooked‚Äù (that is, ready to consume) substance, milk, is derived from its container, the ‚Äúraw‚Äù flesh of the living cow. Now this same milk is going into cups to provide the A≈õvins‚Äô oblation. Similarly, in verse 4 the sweet gharma drink that they produced for the mythological figure Atri comes back to them at this sacrifice. Both of these verses, particularly 3, bristle with difficulties.
With verse 5 the poet turns to his own situation and, in subsequent verses, that of his fellow singers and their patrons. In 5 he presents himself, in the 1st person sin gular, as a poor man beset with difficulties, expressing the hope that with his praise he can bring the A≈õvins to his sacrifice and induce them to make gifts. Verse 6 has as its subject the poet‚Äôs patron, depicted in two punning verbs (6c) as actively involved in the sacrifice; the poet here (6d) hopes that this patron will act in good faith and according to his proper duty by receiving the A≈õvins‚Äô gifts and then redistributing them to the poet and his fellows. Verses 7 and 8 present a series of interlocking causal clauses with shifting subjects. In 7ab because ‚Äúwe singers‚Äù do our job of praising the gods, our patron is not stingy. ‚ÄúWe singers‚Äù (of 7ab) in the 1st person plural transform into Agastya in the 3rd person singular (8cd) via a set of clauses in which the human half of the divine/human interface is left unspecified, and indeed other crucial grammatical information, like the verb in 8ab, is missing. In our inter
pretation, Agastya, who is of course the poet (who appeared in the 1st person sin gular in vs. 5), is the subject of the whole of verse 8, and he is calling the A≈õvins to the Morning Pressing, the sacrifice appropriate to them, whose ritual substance, the soma, is characterized by the difficult hapax v√≠rudra ‚Äúapart from/without Rudra/ the Rudras [=Maruts].‚Äù We believe (after a suggestion of Geldner‚Äôs) that this word encodes a reference to Agastya‚Äôs mythological engagement with the Maruts (see especially the dialogue hymns I.165, 170, 171) and his attempts to ensure that the Maruts get a share in the sacrifice. Here, since the Maruts are associated with the Midday Pressing, the offering of soma to the A≈õvins in the early morning would be made without the Maruts, despite Agastya‚Äôs loyalty to the Maruts and his general eagerness to provide them offerings. In any case, clearly Agastya has had ritual suc cess, crowned both by widespread praise (8c) and by the possession of thousands (presumably of cows or other livestock).

Verses 9‚Äì10 return to the gods‚Äô chariot journey and are relatively straightfor ward, with a particularly uncomplicated demand for horses for us and our patrons in 9cd.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúA≈õvins, your horses are traversers of the (three) regions, when your chariot proceeds to (its desired) quarters; the golden fellies of your wheels grant (whatever is wished for); drinking the Soma, you participate in the morning (rite).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Your easily controlled horses (fly around) the airy realms, when your chariot flies around the floods.
Your golden wheel-rims spray (honey); drinking of the honey you
accompany the dawns.

Your golden fellies scatter drops of moisture: drinking the sweetness ye attend the Mornings.

‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§§‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§§‡§§‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§™‡•Ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡•• ‡§è‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∑‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡•• ‡§â‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Å‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§•‡§É ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§•‡•ã ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ó‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•å ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•É‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§É ‡§≠‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ã‡§∑‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§™‡•å ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ã‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§¨‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§á‡§∑‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§à‡§ü‡•ç‡§ü‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•å‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∞‡§•‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§• ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúDirect downwards (the course) of your (car) swift moving, variously going, friendly to man, and to be especially venerated, when all adorable, your sister (dawn) prepares (for your approach), and (the instrumental tutor of the ceremony) worships you, drinkers of the Soma, for the sake (of obtaining) strength and food.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

You descend to the movement of the steed of wide flight [=ritual fire], the one belonging to men, foremost at the sacrifice,
when your sister [=Dawn] will bring you, o you welcomed by all,
and (the singer) solemnly invokes you for victory‚Äôs prize and for
refreshment, o honey-drinkers.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This is a difficult verse to construe, primarily because of the anomalous y√°d that ends pƒÅda a. It cannot (or should not) mark that pƒÅda as a subordinate clause because the verb nak·π£atha·∏• is unaccented. But if it is taken as marking what immediately follows as a subordinate clause, this is awkward at best, because the gen. phrase of b should simply specify the gen. √°tyasya in a. The Geldner (/Witzel Got≈ç) solution is to supply an acc. goal for nak·π£atha·∏• (‚ÄúSchnelligkeit‚Äù), which is picked up by the y√°d and the following genitive phrase: ‚Äúreach (the speed) of the steed, which (speed) (is) of the ‚Ä¶‚Äù Oldenberg suggests that the y√°d that should subordinate pƒÅda d has simply been stuck in early at the end of pƒÅda a for metrical reasons – an unlikely tyro‚Äôs error for a skilled poet like Agastya and an interpretational hypothesis that essentially tells us that all bets are off in Rigvedic syntax. This is not a worthy representative of Oldenberg‚Äôs usual acumen. Renou suggests either the Geldner solution or an anticipation of the y√°d of c.

There is a much simpler solution, which avoids these syntactic contortions and also avoids the need to supply an acc. goal with nak·π£atha·∏• or to allow √°va ‚àönak·π£ to take a goal in the genitive. The solution is to take y√°d (/y√°t) as the substantivized neuter NA sg. of the present participle to ‚àöi ‚Äògo‚Äô (‚Äògoing‚Äô √† ‚Äòmovement‚Äô); for a similar interpr. see ƒÅy√°t in III.55.8 and also vs. 3c below. It is the goal of the verb, and the genitives of ab are dependent on it; there is then no syntactic break between the pƒÅdas.

Contra Geldner, I do not think that the first member of v√≠patman- is v√≠- ‚Äòbird‚Äô, but, with Grassmann, etc., the preverb v√≠-. The lexeme v√≠ ‚àöpat is found elsewhere, incl. in an Agastya hymn I.168.6.

Geldner (sim. Grassmann, Witzel Got≈ç) suggests that the referent of the genitives is the Sonnenrosse or DadhikrƒÅvan. This does not fit spatially with the √°va ‚Äòdown‚Äô of √°va nak·π£atha·∏•. I think rather of the ritual fire: Agni is often compared to an √°tya-; ‚Äòof wide flight‚Äô would well describe the movements of the newly kindled fire; although I could not identify an unambiguous example of n√°rya- referring to Agni, ‚Äòbelong to men, manly‚Äô is a reasonable description of his role; as is pr√°yajyu- ‚Äòforemost at the sacrifice‚Äô, which is applied to Agni at III.6.2. Reference to the ritual fire also makes sense in the context of the second hemistich where Dawn escorts the A≈õvins to the sacrifice and a ritual officiant (to be supplied) solemnly invokes (ƒ´ÃÅ·π≠·π≠e) them. The root ‚àöƒ´·∏ç is essentially restricted to such ritual situations.

Griffith

Ye as ye travel overtake the Courser who flies apart, the Friend of man, most holy.
The prayer is that the Sister may convey you, all praised, meath-drinkers! to support and strengthen.

Geldner

Ihr erreicht die Schnelligkeit des Renners des wie der Vogel fliegenden, mannhaften, opfersamen, wenn euch, ihr Allgelobten, die Schwester bringen wird und der S√§nger euch, S√º√ütrinker, um Gewinn und Speisung anruft.

Grassmann

Wenn ihr das schnelle Ross der Sonne einholt, das weithin fliegt und allen Menschen Heil bringt, Wenn, allwillkommne, euch die Schwester pflegend, Speis’ und Trank euch Honigtrinker ladet.

(‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑ ‡§ö‡§≤‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§ò‡•ã‡§°‡§º‡•á ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§µ, ‡§®‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§•‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•ã (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§ ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•ã (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ) ‡§¨‡§π‡§ø‡§® ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä (‡§≠‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡•á-‡§™‡•ã‡§∑‡•á (‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø, ‡§ö) ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§à‡§ü‡•ç‡§ü‡•á) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•á (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§™‡•å) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§†‡•á ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•Ä‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§á‡§∑‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§è ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡•• ‡•® ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§ò‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§•‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§∏‡§ï‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§¨‡§π‡§ø‡§® ‡§™‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•ã ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§π‡•ã ? ‡•• ‡•® ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ω‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§≠‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•á‡§ü‡•ç‡§ü‡•á ‡§§‡•å ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§™‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∑‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡§•‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•® ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§Ö‡§µ) (‡§®‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§•‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§•‡§É (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•å (‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§®‡•É‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ã‡§É (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ) ‡§≠‡§ó‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•å (‡§≠‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§ø) ‡§≠‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç (‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§à‡§ü‡•ç‡§ü‡•á) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•å‡§§‡§ø (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§™‡•å) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§ø‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•å (‡§á‡§∑‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§ö) ‡•• ‡•® ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§µ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§•‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§ó‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•ã ‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡•• ‡•® ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúYouhave restored milk to the cow; you have (brought) down the prior mature (secretion) into the unripe (or barren udder) of the cow; the devout offerer of the oblation worships you, whose forms are truth, (as vigilant in the midst of the ceremony), as a thief (in the midst) of a thicket.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

You set milk in the ruddy (cow)‚Äîthe cooked in the raw‚Äî(set) down the cow‚Äôs primordial (substance),
which (milk) is (now) going within the wooden (cups) for you, as a
blazing twisting (snake [=fire]) goes among the trees, o you whose
breath is truth. The one with the oblation is performing sacrifice.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The construction of this vs. is, if anything, even more challenging than the previous one, at least in its second half. As noted in the published introduction, the first half concerns the favorite paradox of the ‚Äúcooked‚Äù milk coming from the raw cow. My only deviation from the standard tr. is to take account of the odd position of √°va in b, which I take to signal a transition from a general statement about the paradoxical nature of milk to a particular statement about the ritual situation. I re-supply adhattam with √°va, but here it refers to the deposit of the ritual milk down on the ritual ground.

The problems lie in the 2nd hemistich, and the knottiest one is posed by the apparent mismatch between the case of the simile and its supposed correspondent in the frame. To allow Geldner‚Äôs tr. to represent the standard, he takes y√°d in c as a neut. acc. referring to the milk of the previous hemistich and functioning as obj. to yajƒÅte in d (roughly, ‚Äúwhich (milk) the oblation-offerer sacrifices to you‚Äù). But the milk is also compared to the undoubted nominative phrase hvƒÅr√≥ n√° ≈õ√∫ci·∏• – a discrepancy he attributes to ‚ÄúDer Nom. statt Akk. im Vergleich,‚Äù a false explanation that I hope I dispatched for good in my 1982 IIJ article on case disharmony in RVic similes (and which Thieme [KlSch 79-80 n. 4 = 1951: 8-9] also excoriates him for, though without an adequate alternative solution in my view). Another problem posed by this interpr. (even for those who deal with the simile in another way) is that it requires ‚Äòmilk‚Äô to be the obj. of y√°jate with vƒÅm then an oblique case, but ‚àöyaj (without preverb) almost always takes an acc. of the divinity and an oblique case (generally instr.) of the offering substance. (Grassmann gives templates with acc. of the offering, but the passages supposedly conforming to them are few and far between.) Again, a more radical approach to the text can eliminate the problems without compromising the grammar. I suggest that y√°jate in d is not the verb of the rel. clause introduced by y√°d in c, but starts a new cl., consisting only of y√°jate hav√≠·π£mƒÅn – a simple statement ending a complex verse. The verb is accented because it opens its clause. What precedes in cd is a nominal clause, with ‚Äòmilk‚Äô as subject. It is possible to assume that there is no verb at all but a gapped copula, but I actually think that there is a haplologized present participle *y√°t following the rel. prn. y√°d (hence y√°d

*y√°d), again, as in vs. 2a, the neut. N/A pres. participle of ‚àöi ‚Äògo‚Äô. Assuming a haplologized monosyllable here fixes the meter, making a 10-syllable line into a proper Tri·π£·π≠ubh, with an opening of 5 and a fine break and cadence. Hence, the milk ‚Äúwhich (y√°d) is going (*y√°t) within (ant√°r) the wooden cups (van√≠na·∏•).‚Äù With milk as a nominative subject, the nominative simile is grammatically impeccable, without (in the mode of Thieme [/Witzel Got≈ç]) having to apply it to the hav√≠·π£mƒÅn, which does not work well. I believe that the ‚Äúblazing twisting‚Äù entity is an image of a snake, but refers to the snaking flames of fire, going into the woods. Thus van√≠na·∏• is read with both frame and simile. In the published translation ‚Äúis (now) going‚Äù should be marked with an asterisk.

Griffith

Ye have deposited, matured within her, in the raw cow the first milk of the milch-cow,
Which the bright offerer, shining like a serpent mid trees, presents to you whose form is perfect.

Geldner

Ihr legtet die Milch in die Kuh, die gekochte in die rohe, den uralten Schatz der Kuh, die euch, ihr Verk√∂rperer der Wahrheit, im Holzgef√§√ü rein wie der Sonnenvogel, der Opferspender darbringt.

Grassmann

Ihr habt die erste Milch der Kuh als gare hineingesetzt in diese rohe Milchkuh; Die Milch, die Agni euch, als bunte Schlange, durch Tr√§nke weiht, o sch√∂ner, bei den Spendern.

Elizarenkova

–í–∞—Ä–µ–Ω–æ–µ ‚Äì –≤ —Å—ã—Ä—É—é, –¥—Ä–µ–≤–Ω–µ–µ (–±–æ–≥–∞—Ç—Å—Ç–≤–æ) –∫–æ—Ä–æ–≤—ã,

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§ã‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç) ‡§ú‡§≤ ‡§ñ‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã ! (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§∂‡•Å‡§ö‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ (‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§∂‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§É) ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡§® ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§â‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ó‡•å ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§™‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§ß ‡§µ‡§æ (‡§Ü‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§ó‡•å ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó (‡§ó‡•ã‡§É) ‡§ó‡•å ‡§ï‡§æ (‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§π‡•à ‡§µ‡§æ (‡§µ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§É) ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∞‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•á (‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É) ‡§≠‡•Ä‡§§‡§∞ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ (‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•á) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§≤ ‡§∞‡§∏ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§Ç‡§ö‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§§‡§æ, ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§ñ‡•á‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ú‡§® ‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§π‡•ã‡§ì ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§ã‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•Å‡§ö‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§® ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ü‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•á‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§â‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ó‡§µ‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§Ü‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•å‡§¢‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§µ) (‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ó‡•ã‡§É) (‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É) (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) (‡§µ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§É) ‡§∞‡§∂‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡§É (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É (‡§ã‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç) ‡§ã‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§§‡•ã ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ø‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•å‡•§ ‡§ã‡§§‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§ï‡§®‡§æ‡•¶‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§Ç‡•¶ ‡•ß‡•§ ‡•ß‡•®‡•§ (‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§É) ‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§É (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§∂‡•Å‡§ö‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§É (‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•á) ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡•á (‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§∂‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•å‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§ø ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï‡•ã‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§ï‡•å ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§≤‡§≠‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§≠‡§µ‡•á‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á‡§§. ‡§ú‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§°‡§≥ ‡§∞‡§∏ ‡§ì‡§¢‡•Ç‡§® ‡§ò‡•á‡§§‡•á ‡§µ ‡§ö‡§Ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§µ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≤‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•ã ‡§§‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï ‡§µ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•á. ‡§ú‡§∏‡•á ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§∂‡§æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§§, ‡§§‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ. ‡•• ‡•© ‡••

English translation:

‚ÄúYou rendered the heat as soothing as sweet butter to Atri; wishing (for relief), and invested it with coolness,like water; therefore, for you, A≈õvins, leaders (of rites) the fire-offering (is made); to you the Soma (drips) run like chariot wheels (down a declivity).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

You chose the honeyed hot drink for Atri, to send it here like a surge of water.
Now (the refreshing drinks) of honey come back to you, like chariot wheels on the quest for livestock, o A≈õvins, superior men.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. also presents a number of difficulties. The easiest to deal with is av·πõ·πáƒ´tam, the 2nd du. active imperfect, to the 9th class pres. to ‚àöv·πõ ‚Äòchoose‚Äô, which is otherwise only middle. We should hypothetically expect middle *av·πõ·πáƒÅthƒÅm, but in fact, acdg. to Macdonell, no athematic present in Vedic attests such a form, whatever the present class. The 2nd /3rd ps. middle dual forms seem to have been avoided. and this active nonce form is probably modeled on 3rd singular medial impf. av·πõ·πáƒ´ta, which is fairly common and occurs a number of times in just this metrical position, after an opening of 5 in trimeter verse. Geldner (p. 258 n. 3) attributes the active voice to the fact that the A≈õvins are choosing on behalf of someone else; this is an ingenious suggestion and merits consideration, but I think the formal problems tipped the balance.

The real crux in this vs. is e·π£√©, which has received almost as many analyses as there are RVic interpretors. For some of the various suggestions see Oldenberg ad loc., Geldner‚Äôs n. 4ab, Renou √âVP XVI ad loc., Scarlatta (60-61), Keydana (Inf. 236 n. 135); there is no space (and I have no patience) to discuss them all here. I take it as a dat. inf. to ‚àöi·π£ ‚Äòsend‚Äô + ƒÅÃÅ, built directly to the root. This root identification may be supported by vs. 6 of the next hymn, I.181.6b p≈´rvƒ´ÃÅr √≠·π£a·∏• ‚Ä¶ m√°dhva i·π£·πá√°n ‚Äúsending many refreshing drinks of honey,‚Äù with similar sense.

I take the simile ap√≥ n√° k·π£√≥da·∏• as obj. of this inf., parallel to the gharma drink that the A≈õvins are sending here. With Grassmann, I take ap√°·∏• as one of the rare sg. forms of √°p- ‚Äòwater‚Äô, preserved here in what is almost a deconstructed compound. For the gen. with k·π£√≥da·∏• cf. Grassmann

Dem Atri habt ihr euren s√ºssen Milchtrank auf seinen Wunsch gew√§hrt, wie Wasserfluten, Drum gehn zu euch, o Ritter, s√ºsse Tr√§nke wie Wagenr√§der, die ihr Milch begehret.

Elizarenkova

‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§®‡§∞‡•å) ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ï ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§¨‡§ø‡§ú‡•Å‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã ! (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§è‡§∑‡•á) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§ì‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è (‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á) ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§, ‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç, ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§§‡•Ä‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ê‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∞‡§π‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§® ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§¶‡§É) ‡§ú‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§™‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§Ö‡§µ‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡§∂‡•Å‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø, (‡§∞‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ) ‡§∞‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ (‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§™‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞ ‡§´‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§§‡§§‡•ç, ‡§π) ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ì ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§™‡§∂‡•Å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã, ‡§∞‡§• ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ã ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§® ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§®‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§Ω‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§¶‡•ã‡§Ω‡§™‡•ã ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ω‡§µ‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§π) ‡§ï‡§ø‡§≤ (‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á) ‡§® ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§£‡§ø ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•à ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§Ö‡§™‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§¶‡§É) ‡§â‡§¶‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§µ‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§è‡§∑‡•á) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§µ‡•á (‡§§‡§§‡•ç) (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É (‡§®‡§∞‡•å) ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ï‡•å (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡•å (‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡§∂‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø‡§É (‡§∞‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ) ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§∞‡§•‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•Ç‡§®‡§ø (‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø) (‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Ç‡§®‡§ø ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç ‡§∞‡§•‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø ‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

English translation:

‚ÄúDasras, may I bring you, by the vehicle of my prayers, (hither), to bestow (upon me your aid), like the infirm son of Tugra; heaven and earth combine (to worship you) through your greatness; may this, your aged (worshipper), adorable (A≈õvins), enjoy long life, (exempt) from sin.‚Äù

With a laud might I turn you here for the giving of a cow, as the
time-worn son of Tugra did, o wondrous ones‚Äî
a great roar (as if) of water accompanies you two great ones‚Äî(might I,) worn out from anxiety and without cattle, (turn) you (here), o you who are worthy of the sacrifice.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Yet another near impossible verse.

The standard tr. take g√≥r √≥he·πáa as a phrase (e.g., Geldner ‚Äúdurch Anpreisung der Kuh(milch)‚Äù). This interpr. is favored by the adjacency of the two words and even more by the retroflexion in √≥he·πáa, which must be triggered by the final -r or g√≥r and speaks for a close syntactic bond. Nonetheless, in the published translation I construe g√≥·∏• with dƒÅnƒÅÃÅya (‚Äúfor the giving of a cow‚Äù), in part because pƒÅda d (in my interpr.) identifies the poet as lacking cattle and in part because ‚Äúby praise of the cow‚Äù doesn‚Äôt make much sense as a way to attract the A≈õvins. However, the close sandhi of g√≥r √≥he·πáa gives me pause, and I might change the published translation to ‚ÄúWith a laud of the cow might I turn you here for giving.‚Äù Thieme gives a complex, sensitive, and in many way appealing treatment of this vs. in KZ 92 (1978): 40-42. In the first hemistich he takes dƒÅnƒÅÃÅya as ambiguous, between ‚Äúfor (our) giving (to you)‚Äù and ‚Äúfor (your) giving (to us).‚Äù The ambiguity is also reflected in his double reading of g√≥r√≥he·πáa: with a division g√≥ r√≥he·πáa (already suggested by Pischel), this can mean ‚Äúby the rising of the milk‚Äù and refer to the boiling up of the milk offered to the A≈õvins in the Pravargya ritual. With a division g√≥r √≥he·πáa it simply means ‚Äúmit dem Ruf eines Rinde‚Äù and refers to the loud cry with which Bhujyu summoned the A≈õvins. Given Agastya‚Äôs seemingly limitless verbal trickery, Thieme‚Äôs suggested double readings are certainly possible, though I wonder if g√≥r √≥he·πáa needs to be confined to the simile, rather than referring to the cry with which we attract the A≈õvins.

The second hemistich is more contested. Most tr. take k·π£o·πáƒ´ÃÅas dual acc, which it of course can be, with the fem. adj. mƒÅÃÅhinƒÅ the subject and wanting a referent: e.g., Geldner (/Witzel Got≈ç) ‚Äòhelp‚Äô (≈´t√≠·∏•). I instead follow Thieme (and in fact Grassmann) in taking k·π£o·πáƒ´ÃÅhere as nom. sg., and I read mƒÅÃÅhinƒÅ in two ways, as nom. sg. with k·π£o·πáƒ´ÃÅ and dual acc. with vƒÅm (Thieme only the latter). Thieme also takes all of the 2nd hemistich as referring to the story of Bhujyu, the A≈õvins‚Äô client whom they saved from the sea. This allows him to take ap√°·∏• as ablative sg.: ‚Äúa cry from the water,‚Äù where Bhujyu was languishing. I believe the Bhujyu reference is confined to pƒÅda b, though Agastya‚Äôs extensive treatment of the story in nearby I.182.5-7 gives me pause and might lead me to reconsider. Meanwhile I take ap√°·∏• in c as gen. sg., as in 4b. Note the similarity between 4b ap√≥ n√° k·π£√≥da·∏• and 5c ap√°·∏• k·π£o·πáƒ´ÃÅ. In my interpr., pƒÅda c is parenthetical, describing the noise that attends the A≈õvins‚Äô journey, produced both by their quick progress in the chariot and by the cries of us ritualists seeking to bring them to us. I then take d as a return to the 1st sg. speaker of a(b) and supply the verb from pƒÅda a, ƒÅÃÅ‚Ä¶ vav·πõtƒ´ya ‚Äúmight I turn you here.‚Äù My interpr.

depends crucially on Thieme‚Äôs ingenious (and to me convincing) analysis of √°k·π£u- as ‚Äòwithout cattle‚Äô, formed in opposition to k·π£um√°nt- ‚Äòhaving cattle‚Äô, and containing an underlying *p(a)≈õu-. (See EWA s.v. √°k·π£u2 ; Mayrhofer accepts this analysis.) The standard tr. are founded on √°k·π£u- ‚Äònet‚Äô, found several times in the AV; Thieme allows the possibility of a second reading with the ‚Äònet‚Äô word, which seems a bit stretched.

  • ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Æ‡•à‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§É
  • ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ü‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§™‡•ç

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã ! (‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§É) ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß (‡§§‡•å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§¨‡§≤‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§® ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç (‡§ó‡•ã‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡•á‡§£) ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§® ‡§∏‡•á (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§¶‡•á‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§Ü‡§µ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø) ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á (‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§¨‡§°‡§º‡•Ä ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§£‡•Ä) ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§™‡§É) ‡§ú‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ (‡§∏‡§ö‡§§‡•á) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á (‡§ú‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§É) ‡§∞‡•ã‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç‡§Å ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É) ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§π‡§∏‡§É) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§ó ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§ñ‡•Ç‡§Å ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ú‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ê‡§∏‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§ó ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§ñ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§®‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•ã‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡•á‡§£ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§É ‡§∏‡§ö‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ú‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§Ω‡§π‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ö‡•á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç‡§π‡§∏‡•ã ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ü) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø) (‡§µ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø) ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ (‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡•ã‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•å (‡§ó‡•ã‡§É) ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É (‡§ì‡§π‡•á‡§®) ‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§®‡•á‡§® (‡§§‡•å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§§‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§¨‡§≤‡§ø‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§≠‡§µ‡§É (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§ú‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§É) ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•ã ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§É (‡§Ö‡§™‡§É) ‡§ú‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§£‡•Ä) ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§É (‡§∏‡§ö‡§§‡•á) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§ß‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡§ø (‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ú‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§É) ‡§∞‡•ã‡§ó‡•Ä (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É) ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§É (‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§π‡§∏‡§É) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•å ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•å‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ã ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§è‡§µ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ø‡•á‡§Æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•ã‡§™‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á‡§§. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§® ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ, ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§†‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡•ã, ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§® ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞ ‡§†‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡•ã ‡§§‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡•Ä‡§π‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡•á‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§ú‡•á ‡§µ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•á ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ ‡§™‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•á‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§ú‡•á. ‡•• ‡•´ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúBountiful givers, when you yoke your horses you replenish the earth with viands; may this your worshipper be (swift) as the wind to propitiate and plural ase you, that, through your great (favour), he may, like a pious man, obtain food.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

When you team up your teams, o you of good gifts, you send abundance nearby by your own autonomous powers.
Our patron gives the ritual prompt [/will please]; he toils [/will pursue] like the wind. Like one who well follows his commandments, he has taken victory‚Äôs prize, in order (to show) great (generosity?).

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, I take the 2nd hemistich as a punning depiction of the poet‚Äôs patron, anchored by two adjacent and rhyming verbs that have double readings, pr√©·π£ad v√©·π£ad. The former is generally taken as the s-aor. subj. to ‚àöprƒ´ ‚Äòplease‚Äô (Wh Rts, Grassmann, Narten [176], as well as the standard tr.), but it could also be assigned to pr√° ‚àöi·π£ ‚Äòsend forth‚Äô (pace Renou, who explicitly rejects this analysis) and refer to the prai·π£a- ‚Äòritual prompt‚Äô, a technical term in the later ritual but already reflected in the RV, at least in my view (cf. purupra√≠·π£a- I.145.3 and comm. ad loc.). I think that both readings are present. Those who assign the form to ‚àöprƒ´ interpret it as an unusual intransitive / reflexive (‚Äúbecomes pleased, pleases himself‚Äù), in contrast to the standard transitive use of the active forms of this root. But this is unnecessary.

The subject is the ritual patron (s≈´r√≠-), whose function is to distribute largesse to the poet and priests. It is used without object here to enable both the ‚àöprƒ´ and the pr√° ‚àöi·π£ senses to be actualized. The same goes for the next verb v√©·π£at. This latter verb is generally taken also as an s-aor. subjunctive, to ‚àövƒ´ ‚Äòpursue‚Äô (e.g., Grassmann, Narten [246], as well as the standard tr.), and I agree that this is one of its readings. But I would also take it as an injunc. to ‚àövi·π£ ‚Äòtoil‚Äô, two forms of which are found nearby (I.178.2, 181.6). The two injunctive forms ‚Äúgives the prompt and toils‚Äù express activities strictly limited to ritual performance; the two subjunctives ‚Äúwill please and will pursue‚Äù remain tied to the ritual but express its larger goals: ‚Äúwill please (the poet and priests) and will pursue (the gods).‚Äù PƒÅda d expresses the redistribution of goods that characterizes the Vedic ritual system. The patron acquires goods, hence the ƒÅÃÅ‚Ä¶ dade ‚Äòhas taken‚Äô; in this case the vƒÅÃÅja- ‚Äòprize‚Äô must be the ‚Äòabundance‚Äô (p√∫ra·πÉdhi-) sent by the A≈õvins in b. The gods give these goods in response to the praises produced by the poets. The patron then distributes these acquisitions to his clients, here the ritual functionaries who prompted the gods‚Äô gifts in the first place. There are several possible grammatical analyses of mah√©, but the dominant mah√© is the dat. sg. to the athematic stem m√°h-.

Given the patronage situation depicted, I take it as short for mah√© *rƒÅÃÅdhase ‚Äúfor great (generosity),‚Äù as in I.139.6, II.41.6=VI.45.27, VIII.2.29, 24.10, 45.24, 64.12, 93.16.

(‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§É) ‡§™‡§µ‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§ ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡•á‡§•‡•á) ‡§è‡§ï ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•ã ‡§§‡§¨ (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á (‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§â‡§™, ‡§∏‡•É‡§ú‡§•‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•ã ‡§µ‡§π (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡•ã (‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§É) ‡§™‡§µ‡§® ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§ì‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§É) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•Ç‡§≤ ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡§® ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§Æ‡§π‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§¨‡§°‡§º‡§™‡•ç‡§™‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑ ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ü‡§¶‡§¶‡•á) ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ç‡§Å ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§≤‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§ ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ö‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§™‡§µ‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§ó ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§Å-‡§§‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ã ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡•á‡§•‡•á ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§∏‡•É‡§ú‡§•‡§É ‡§∏ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ã ‡§® ‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ã ‡§® ‡§Æ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§¶‡•á ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§®‡§ø) (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡•á‡§•‡•á) ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§•‡§É (‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§É) ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§∏‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Ç) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•å (‡§â‡§™) (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•à‡§É (‡§∏‡•É‡§ú‡§•‡§É) (‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•§ ‡§≤‡•á‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡§ø‡•§ (‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡•Å‡•§ ‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡§ø ‡§≤‡•á‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É‡•§ (‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§É) ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§Ü) (‡§Æ‡§π‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•á (‡§¶‡§¶‡•á) (‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§É) ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡•å‡§∂‡§≤‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡§ï‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§É ‡§ö‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§ó‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§≠‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á. ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§≤‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡•Ä. ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á‡§≤‡•á‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§µ ‡§ï‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ö‡§æ‡§≤‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•á‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§ó‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§ø‡§§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§æ‡§µ‡•á. ‡•• ‡•¨ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúYour sincere adorers, we verily praise you in various ways. The pitcher has been plural ced (ready for the Soma). Irreproachable A≈õvins, showerers (of benefits) drink freely of the juice in the presence of the gods.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The pitcher has been plural ced: vipa·πáir-hitƒÅvƒÅn: pa·πái = dro·πáa kala≈õa, the jar or vessel into which the Soma, after expression, is strained through a filter; pa·πái may also mean a merchant or trader;

HitƒÅvƒÅn = being plural ced or opulent;

Vi = viyujyatƒÅm, let him be separated; the imlication is: let the niggardly rich man who does not offer worship be cast off; another reading is: ƒÅhavi·∏• pa·πái = a niggard, offering no oblation

Jamison Brereton

Since we, your trusty singers, are expressing our admiration for you, the niggard with his hoard is far away.
And since, o A≈õvins, (you are) irreproachable, because you protect (the man) who has the gods nearby, o bulls,

Jamison Brereton Notes

The nonce verbal form vipanyƒÅÃÅmahe has attracted a remarkable amount of discussion; for a detailed and clear summary see Kulikov (-ya-presents, 143-46), though his passive/reciprocal interpr. I cannot follow. Insofar as there has been a standard analysis of it, it has been as a passive to ‚àöpan ‚Äòadmire‚Äô (Wh Rts, reflected in Geldner‚Äôs tr.), but other root associations and morphological analyses abound, which I will not discuss further. It belongs with a group of other, better-attested formations: fem. noun vipanyƒÅÃÅ- and adj. vipany√∫-, which I take as ‚Äòadmiration‚Äô and ‚Äòseeking/expressing admiration‚Äô respectively. (This point was made forcefully by Thieme in Fs. Risch [1986: 165-66], though I do not follow his ultimately etymology.) (For a similar system, cf. vas≈´y√°-(ti), vas≈´yƒÅÃÅ-, vas≈´y√∫-.) I take vipanyƒÅÃÅmahe as a denominative -y√°-verb associated with these nominal forms, derived from the root ‚àöpan (in my view); it is transitive in value and takes vƒÅm as object. With Thieme (an analysis fld. by subsequent interpr. Kulikov Witzel Got≈ç), I take the last part of pƒÅda b, v√≠ pa·πá√≠r hitƒÅÃÅvƒÅn, as a separate nominal clause, but unlike these interpr. I take it as the main clause attached to the causal clause beginning in pƒÅda a.

The phonological play of vipan(yƒÅÃÅmahe) v√≠ pa·πá(√≠r) clearly contributed to the word choice here, as Kulikov also notes. As for the sense of this hemistich, it simply rephrases the purport of vs. 6: because we singers are doing our job in the ritual economy by praising the gods, our patron is being generous to us and cannot be labeled a ‚Äòniggard‚Äô.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The poet then turns to the A≈õvins‚Äô part in this system and in 7cd presents another causal clause, parallel to the one in ab. Note the parallel openings 7a vay√°·πÉ cid dh√≠ and 7c √°dhƒÅ cid dh√≠. This subordinated causal expression continues into vs. 8, with another parallel causal clause occupying 8ab and introduced in a similar way: 8a yuvƒÅÃÅ·πÉ cid dh√≠. In my, admittedly complex, interpr. of these verses, the 1st plural singers of 7ab modulate into the single (3rd ps.) singer Agastya in 8cd; this modulation is eased by not naming him until we reach the triumphant main clause of 8cd, where he boasts of the great wealth he has acquired from his poetry – just like the singers of 7ab. And the transition from 1st ps. singers to 3rd ps. singer is enabled by omitting both subj. and verb in 8ab, where a human ritualist or ritualists should be the agents (see below) but where the ps. and no. of any verb would be problematically telltale.

The series of causal clauses begins by highlighting the A≈õvins‚Äô benevolent participation in the ritual system (7cd) and then the complementary activity of the priest in summoning them to the sacrifice and offering to them first (8ab) – before coming to the logical conclusion in 8cd: that Agastya has achieved his just reward and is himself acclaimed among men.

With the standard tr. I take anindyƒÅ in 7c as a predicted voc., with c and d separate clauses, as the double h√≠ suggests.

Griffith

For verily we truthful singers praise you the niggard trafficker is here excluded.
Now, even now do ye O blameless Asvins, ye Mighty, guard the man whose God is near him.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ú‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§ñ‡•á‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á‡§Ω‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§£‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡•á‡§Æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§ú‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§π‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§•‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§ß ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø (‡§π‡§ø) (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ú‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§æ‡§É (‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§µ‡§É (‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§π‡•á) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§π‡•á (‡§µ‡§ø) (‡§™‡§£‡§ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§π‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ (‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§É (‡§Ö‡§ß) ‡§Ö‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§®‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§É‡•§ (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§π‡§ø) ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•Å (‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§è‡§µ (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å (‡§Ö‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•å (‡§™‡§æ‡§•‡§É) ‡§â‡§¶‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§π‡§ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§Ö‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§®‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§É‡•§ (‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§£‡•å) ‡§¨‡§≤‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•å (‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•å‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á‡§§. ‡§ú‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§® ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§§ ‡§µ ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ç‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§§, ‡§§‡§∏‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ. ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

Jamison Brereton Notes

Based on my interpr. of the structure of vss. 7-8, I supply Agastya as the subject of 8ab, which not only lacks an overt subject but also an overt verb, for the reasons sketched above. Geldner supplies ‚Äú(opferte er),‚Äù Witzel Got≈ç ‚Äú(ruft man an)‚Äù; I favor something like the latter (my ‚Äòsummons‚Äô), on the somewhat fragile basis that yuvƒÅÃÅm is fairly frequently the obj. of ‚àöh≈´ ‚Äòcall, summon‚Äô (cf., e.g., I.47.4=VIII.5.17 yuvƒÅÃÅ·πÉ havante a≈õvinƒÅ).

Curiously enough pr√° does not appear with verbal forms of ‚àösru ‚Äòflow‚Äô, but the preverb probably indexes the ritual fact that A≈õvins receive their offering at the Morning Pressing, the ritual event that leads off the soma offerings of the day.

v√≠rudra- is a hapax and difficult (but what is not in this hymn?), but with Geldner, etc., I take it as a bahuvrƒ´hi ‚Äòhaving Rudra (or rather the Rudras=Maruts) away‚Äô, referring to soma. As Geldner hints (n. 8b), this probably is a reference to the Agastya - Indra - Marut cycle that forms such a dramatic part of Agastya‚Äôs oeuvre. It would refer in part simply to the fact that this is the Morning Pressing, and the Maruts receive their soma at the Midday Pressing. But also more specifically to the fact that whether the Maruts should have any part in the soma sacrifice and whether in particular they should have a share in Indra‚Äôs part were fraughtly disputed in those hymns. Agastya is in effect pointing out that there was never any question of the A≈õvins having to share with the Maruts. (That by some accounts the A≈õvins only got included in the soma sacrifice belatedly might make this a question that would exercise them.) The actual form, v√≠rudra-, is reminiscent of the nominal clause v√≠ pa·πá√≠·∏• ‚Ä¶ ‚Äúthe niggard is away‚Äù in 7b.

PƒÅda c is fairly straightforward, and it is worth noting that pr√° ‚àö≈õa·πÉs here is used of a human (though not a king, unfortunately), reflecting what I think is its original domain. There is some debate about the value of citayat, which belongs to the functionally malleable stem cit√°ya-. But in the absence of anything that could serve as an object, I take it as the intransitive it regularly is, ‚Äòappear, be conspicuous‚Äô.

As for sah√°srai·∏•, everywhere else where it‚Äôs clear, this instr. pl. refers to cows or other countable forms of wealth (so also Witzel Got≈ç in n.), and I take it as indicating the reason for Agastya‚Äôs perceived prominence.

Yet another hapax troubles us: kƒÅÃÅrƒÅdhunƒ´. Both Geldner and Witzel Got≈ç refuse to tr. it.

But given the other intractable problems in this hymn, it seems one of the lesser issues. The 2nd member -dhuni- is surely dh√∫ni- ‚Äònoisy‚Äô or probably, substantivized ‚Äònoise, tumult‚Äô. It also seems reasonably possible to connect kƒÅÃÅrƒÅ- to kƒÅr√∫- ‚Äòbard‚Äô and assign it a tenative meaning ‚Äòpraise-song‚Äô (so also EWA s.v. kƒÅr√∫-, citing also AiG II.2, etc.). The form in the text (with probable but not certain -ƒ´ final, so Pp.) can be an instr. sg. The remaining problem is the accent, which should make the cmpd a bahuvrƒ´hi, a grammatical identity at odds with my tr. ‚Äúwith a tumult of praise-songs.‚Äù AiG II.1.221 simply says it has abnormal accent, and with nothing better to offer, I will simply allow Wackernagel the last word on that subject. I take this simile with what precedes (‚Äúproclaimed as if with ‚Ä¶‚Äù) rather than what follows (‚Äúis conspicuous by thousands as if by ‚Ä¶‚Äù). Jamison Brereton Notes

The poet then turns to the A≈õvins‚Äô part in this system and in 7cd presents another causal clause, parallel to the one in ab. Note the parallel openings 7a vay√°·πÉ cid dh√≠ and 7c √°dhƒÅ cid dh√≠. This subordinated causal expression continues into vs. 8, with another parallel causal clause occupying 8ab and introduced in a similar way: 8a yuvƒÅÃÅ·πÉ cid dh√≠. In my, admittedly complex, interpr. of these verses, the 1st plural singers of 7ab modulate into the single (3rd ps.) singer Agastya in 8cd; this modulation is eased by not naming him until we reach the triumphant main clause of 8cd, where he boasts of the great wealth he has acquired from his poetry – just like the singers of 7ab. And the transition from 1st ps. singers to 3rd ps. singer is enabled by omitting both subj. and verb in 8ab, where a human ritualist or ritualists should be the agents (see below) but where the ps. and no. of any verb would be problematically telltale.

The series of causal clauses begins by highlighting the A≈õvins‚Äô benevolent participation in the ritual system (7cd) and then the complementary activity of the priest in summoning them to the sacrifice and offering to them first (8ab) – before coming to the logical conclusion in 8cd: that Agastya has achieved his just reward and is himself acclaimed among men.

With the standard tr. I take anindyƒÅ in 7c as a predicted voc., with c and d separate clauses, as the double h√≠ suggests.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•Æ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§§‡§µ‡§®‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§®‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§®‡•É‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§®‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç ‡•• ‡•Æ ‡••

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) (‡§π‡§ø) ‡§Ø‡§§‡§É (‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ) (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å) ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å (‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ ‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§ó‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§∏‡§æ‡§§‡•å) ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•å (‡§Ö‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§Æ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§É (‡§®‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§®‡•É‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§É (‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§®‡•Ä‡§µ) ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ß‡•Ç‡§®‡§Ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§µ (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç (‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§É) ‡•• ‡•Æ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•å‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ω‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•• ‡•Æ ‡••

‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§π‡•á‡§•‡•á ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§•‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§∞‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§π‡§§ ‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§•‡§É ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡•á ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§•‡§É ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•å ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§•‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡•§ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡•á ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡§™‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡•É‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§´‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§â‡§§ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å ‡§≠‡§µ‡•å ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡•§ ‡§® ‡§Ö‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•å ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§®‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∏‡§æ‡§ö‡§É ‡§ß‡§®‡§∏‡§Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§≠‡§µ‡•á‡§Æ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen, by the virtue of your chariot, you come (to the sacrifice), and when, like the ministering priest (after discharging his rfunction), you depart, gliding away, you give to the devout (worshippers a reward), thogh it be store of good horses; may we be enriched, NƒÅsatyƒÅs (by your favour).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

When by the greatness of your chariot you carry yourselves forward, you drive forth like the Hotar [=Agni] of Manu, o streaming ones. Provide an abundance of good horses to our patrons and (to us): might we be companions of wealth, o NƒÅsatyas.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The phrase s≈´r√≠bhya ut√° vƒÅ is elliptical. Klein (DGRV II.171) suggests supplying ‚Äòsingers‚Äô, while I prefer ‚Äòus‚Äô. Either is possible. When with the glory of your car ye travel, when we go speeding like the priest of mortals,

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ö‡§≤‡§®‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä-‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã ! (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ (‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ) ‡§¶‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§¨‡§°‡§º‡§™‡•ç‡§™‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§æ‡§• (‡§∞‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∞‡§• ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§π‡•á‡§•‡•á) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§æ‡§•‡§É) ‡§è‡§ï ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§™‡§π‡•Å‡§Å‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•ã ‡§µ‡•á ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã (‡§â‡§§, ‡§µ‡§æ) ‡§Ö‡§•‡§µ‡§æ (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞ ‡§ò‡•ã‡§°‡§º‡§æ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ì ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó (‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡§É) ‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§æ‡§• ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ) ‡§π‡•ã‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•Ø ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ú‡§ø‡§® ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§®‡•ç‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§î‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡•á‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡•Å‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡§¢‡§º‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ß‡§®‡§¶‡§æ‡§® ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ß‡§®‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡•• ‡•Ø ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã ‡§® ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§∞‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§π‡•á‡§•‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§æ‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•å ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡•• ‡•Ø ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π ‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞) (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•ã (‡§µ‡§π‡•á‡§•‡•á) (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§•‡§É) (‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π (‡§∞‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞) (‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ö‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡•å (‡§Ø‡§æ‡§•‡§É) ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§•‡§É (‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ) ‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡§æ (‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ß‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É (‡§â‡§§) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø (‡§µ‡§æ) (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§®‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡•å (‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡§É) ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§£‡§æ ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ) ‡§≠‡§µ‡•á‡§Æ ‡•• ‡•Ø ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§™‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§¶‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•• ‡•Ø ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWe invoke today, A≈õvins, with hymns, for the sake of good things, your praiseworthy chariot of undamaged wheels, and traversing the sky, that we may obtain (from it) food, strength and long life.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Today, o A≈õvins, we would call your chariot here with praise songs for ever newer good faring‚Äî
(the chariot) that speeds around heaven with undamaged fellies. ‚Äì May we know refreshment and a community having lively waters.

That circles heaven with never-injured fellies. May we find strengthening food in full abundance.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É (‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§Ö‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§®‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§É‡•§ (‡§π‡•Å‡§µ‡•á‡§Æ) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ (‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Æ‡•à‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ) ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡•å ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•å (‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§®‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É (‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ü‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§á‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ (‡§á‡§∑‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡•É‡§ú‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ú‡•Ä‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•ß‡•¶ ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§Ω‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡•á‡§®‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç ‡•• ‡•ß‡•¶ ‡••‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§§‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§®‡§µ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∏ ‡§®‡•á‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§ú‡•á. ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§≥‡•á ‡§ú‡§ó‡§æ‡§§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§µ ‡§Ü‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§µ‡•Ä. ‡•• ‡•ß‡•¶ ‡••
